  <dd>HyperText Markup Language</dd>

 ^tGuCsCsI

<dl>: Root container

<dt>: Defines the term

<dd>: Provides the definition(s) ^1n3FCffd

Nesting <li> instead of <dt>/<dd> inside <dl>

Using <dl> for non-definition content (e.g., generic item lists)

Failing to pair <dt> with a corresponding <dd>, causing semantic ambiguity ^YZRJBqDU

Versus <ul>/<ol>: <dl> is used for term-definition pairs, while <ul> and <ol> are for general item lists.

<dt> introduces the term; <dd> provides the description. There is no <li> in a <dl>. ^2HjA2tMk

The <dl>, <dt>, and <dd> elements in HTML define a semantic structure for description (or definition) lists. The <dl> element serves as a container for grouping terms and their corresponding definitions. Within this container, each <dt> (definition term) element specifies a term or name, while the subsequent <dd> (definition description) element provides its definition or explanation. The pairing of <dt> and <dd> elements allows for multiple terms to share the same description or for a single term to be associated with multiple explanations. This structure supports content that resembles glossaries, metadata lists, or any key-value style presentation.

These elements are purely semantic and do not impose default visual formatting similar to unordered or ordered lists. Instead, they allow developers to convey relationships between terms and definitions to both browsers and assistive technologies, promoting accessibility and structured data presentation. ^0uMSOyKJ

Distinctions ^MybUHSLH

<dl>
  <dt>Coffee</dt>
  <dd>A brewed drink prepared from roasted coffee beans.</dd>
  <dd>Can be served hot or cold.</dd>
</dl>
